She enjoyed watching football, eating chocolates and cracking jokes, right until the last few weeks of her life.		If you believe in the life after, no doubt she‚Äôs doing those very things right now.
Ann experienced her own difficulties with her health and mobility. But nothing robbed her of her powerful memories, her strong opinions and cutting humor.
She took on an enormous task at a time when most couples their age were busy enjoying retirement and grandchildren. She stayed by his side until the end.
Ann spent years caring for her husband, Roger. First, as he fought cancer in midlife, then in later years as he gradually slipped away to Alzheimer‚Äôs.
By the time Ann held her young great-granddaghter, she could no longer offer material things. She‚Äôd become frail. But a loving embrace she could still give.
Ann plied her grandchildren and nephews and niece with Christmas gifts and birthday cards stuffed with money and showered them with love and affection.
When Roger first brought home Y Lan, his future wife and mother of his children, Jettie liked her immediately. Ann didn‚Äôt always value Jettie‚Äôs opinion. But this one meant something.
Ann depended on Jettie to watch young Roger when he came home from school. Roger learned a great deal from his grandmother, inspiring his four-decade career teaching history.
There was that time the neighbor‚Äôs cows broke through the fence and started feasting on Jettie‚Äôs flowers. Phone in one hand, rifle in the other, Ann took care of business.
Jettie Dees Tate depended on her daughter-in-law. For doctor‚Äôs visits. For trips to Texas to see her relatives. For that color TV set she watched with the volume turned up so loud, you could hear it from the mailbox.
Now, the part where she lived with her mother-in-law. Two strong-willed Southern women under one roof? What could go wrong? Well, plenty.		Though they sometimes clashed, they worked as a team in other ways.
When her eyes got wide, as if to say, ‚Äòdon‚Äôt come for me,‚Äô you best didn‚Äôt. 		No matter your social standing, she‚Äôd put you in your place before you even knew what hit you.
She wasn‚Äôt as gentle, though, when it came to protecting her loved ones, be they her parents, her brothers and sister, her husband, her mother-in-law, her son or her grandchildren. Those who crossed her did so at their own risk.
Sure, it might be your clothes, your hair, your weight or your career choices. But it was never meant to hurt your feelings.
She was a mother, a grandmother, a great-grandmother, a sister and an aunt.		Annie Lou Reeves Tate had a biting wit. If you found yourself on the receiving end of it, you weren‚Äôt alone. A gentle insult became an expression of love.
A caregiver to her mother-in-law for 26 years. A caregiver to her husband for 57 years		The planner and host of family gatherings, with lots of cars in the driveway, where everyone was well-fed, in good times and bad.
She earned the trust of one company so much managing the payroll that another one hired her by reputation after the first went out of business.
Like many women in postwar America, she went to work. She worked hard for the income to pay for college tuition, cars, vacations and Christmas presents.
Ann caught one of the two most eligible bachelors in town. (Roger‚Äôs brother, Thomas, was the other.)
Like many women in postwar America, she fell for and married a handsome war hero. Together, Ann and Roger Tate looked like young Hollywood movie stars.
She was the daughter of an electrician and a homemaker and one of five siblings, four of whom lived past childhood and three of whom still live.
She was a guardian. An unflinching protector of who and what she loved.		She was part of a sturdy generation who came up in depression and war.
If I may take the opportunity to pay tribute to my paternal grandmother, Annie Lou Reeves Tate, February 8, 1931 - December 17, 2018
